    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<span id="freeTextContainer4860474135979974117">Everyone in the small town of Central City, Texas loves Lou Ford. A deputy sheriff, Lou's known to the small-time criminals, the real-estate entrepreneurs, and all of his coworkers--the low-lifes, the big-timers, and everyone in-between--as the nicest guy around. He may not be the brightest or the most interesting man in town, but nevertheless, he's the kind of officer you</span>
  <span id="freeText4860474135979974117" style="display:none">Everyone in the small town of Central City, Texas loves Lou Ford. A deputy sheriff, Lou's known to the small-time criminals, the real-estate entrepreneurs, and all of his coworkers--the low-lifes, the big-timers, and everyone in-between--as the nicest guy around. He may not be the brightest or the most interesting man in town, but nevertheless, he's the kind of officer you're happy to have keeping your streets safe. The sort of man you might even wish your daughter would end up with someday.<br /><br />But behind the platitudes and glad-handing lurks a monster the likes of which few have seen. An urge that has already claimed multiple lives, and cost Lou his brother Mike, a self-sacrificing construction worker who fell to his death on the job in what was anything but an accident. A murder that Lou is determined to avenge--and if innocent people have to die in the process, well, that's perfectly all right with him.<br /><br />In <i>The Killer Inside Me</i>, Thompson goes where few novelists have dared to go, giving us a pitch-black glimpse into the mind of the American Serial Killer years before Charles Manson, John Wayne Gacy, and Brett Easton Ellis's <i>American Psycho</i>, in the novel that will forever be known as the master performance of one of the greatest crime novelists of all time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4860474135979974117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/56347454-sir-gawain"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1464656205i/56347454._UY100_CR20,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I'm not 100% sure, but I got the impression "shebby" was meant to mean "like Queen Sheba". I'm not a big bible guy, but I think Sheba approached King <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I'm not 100% sure, but I got the impression "shebby" was meant to mean "like Queen Sheba". I'm not a big bible guy, but I think Sheba approached King Solomon and asked him some tough questions. So it sounds like Lou thinks he's the king and this queen is questioning him and his actions. Or making him answer her for something anyway.<br />"Sister of love" means prostitute.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1424975903" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1424975903" />
    <a title="Patrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/922495-patrick"><img alt="Patrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1424975903?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick" href="/user/show/922495-patrick">Patrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/922495-patrick?shelf=books-i-would-blurb">books-i-would-blurb</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1424975903" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6910135017795765451">This book was recommended to me by someone who worked in the publishing industry, what's more, they liked my book, so I was pretty sure they had excellent taste. I bought it almost immediately, and was excited to give it a try. <br/><br/>That was almost exactly nine years ago. (This might give you a dim glimmer as to what my to-read shelf is like.)<br/><br/>A couple days ago, I was in-between books and looking over my shelves for something I could read before going to bed. I didn't want to start up another Pratchet</span>
  <span id="freeText6910135017795765451" style="display:none">This book was recommended to me by someone who worked in the publishing industry, what's more, they liked my book, so I was pretty sure they had excellent taste. I bought it almost immediately, and was excited to give it a try. <br /><br />That was almost exactly nine years ago. (This might give you a dim glimmer as to what my to-read shelf is like.)<br /><br />A couple days ago, I was in-between books and looking over my shelves for something I could read before going to bed. I didn't want to start up another Pratchett novel because I was hoping to to bed early. And starting a book that I love is going to cost me sleep.<br /><br />So I pulled this off the shelf instead. Big mistake. <br /><br />Six hours later I finished it, and I've been dealing with the after-effects of too little sleep ever since. <br /><br />I'm not going to describe the book to you. It's something you really need to experience on your own. But I will say this.<br /><br />It was written in 1952. And I found myself thinking, "Wow. I didn't know folks were writing dark shit like this back then." Then I realized what a dumb thought that was.<br /> <br />I think its genre is "Crime Fiction" but again, that was fifty years ago. I don't know where it would be placed these days. Psychological thriller? <br /><br />It's one of the best uses of first-person narration I've ever seen. <br /><br />I found it incredibly emotionally engaging. Even slightly distressing. But at no point was it gruesome or gratuitous. <br /><br />If you enjoy shows like Dexter. I'd say this book should be an absolute read for you. But honestly, I'd recommend this to anyone who appreciates good writing. <br /><br />Dealbreakers: If you've got an issue with violence, this isn't the book for you. <br /><br />But again, I'd like to stress that there isn't a lot of it in here. Game of Thrones has vastly more and it's vastly more graphic. This book just has a bit, but as I've said, it's just… intense, it's not slashery at all. <br /><br />Really amazingly good book. I'll absolutely be digging up more by this author. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6910135017795765451" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4780148855041646986">Stephen King said about novelist Jim Thompson: “He was crazy. He went running into the American subconscious with a blowtorch in one hand and a pistol in the other, screaming his goddamn head off. No one else came close.”<br/><br/>I thought I would love this book, and I did somewhat. I feel kinda dirty after reading it though. Deputy Sheriff Lou Ford is well..he is a fucker. He hides in plain sight. That calm deputy that draws no attention to himself, but deep inside his monster's lurk.<br/><a href='http://imgur.com/SSwAjfV' rel='nofollow noopener'>

No one else came close.”<br><br>I thought I would love this book, and I did somewhat. I feel kinda dirty after reading it though. Deputy Sheriff Lou Ford is well..he is a fucker. </a><br>Told from the first person this story draws you in and spits you out at the end. It's not a long book, I finished it in just a few hours. That's honestly about all I could take, any longer and I would have screamed for mercy.<br>Lou likes smacking around and spanking his women. (I hated him immensely at that point) then you find out some of his history and kinda feel sorry for him. <br>Then the monster rears his ugly head again and you want a shower and mind bleach because you felt sorrow for this F@#CKER!!!!!!<br>I'm a pretty jaded reader. I can't even begin to imagine if I had read this book when it first came out. Things like this just didn't exist then. It was way before it's time. <div id="review_116837774" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116837774" />
    <a title="Dan Schwent" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent"><img alt="Dan Schwent" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593973948p2/870755.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/116837774?book_show_action=true">Aug 14, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan Schwent" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan Schwent" href="/user/show/870755-dan-schwent">Dan Schwent</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=pants-shittingly-awesome">pants-shittingly-awesome</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=crime-and-mystery">crime-and-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=cool-covers">cool-covers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=thompson">thompson</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=birhday-2010">birhday-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/870755-dan-schwent?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116837774" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7215691641152538913">Ever meet someone at a party and think they're pretty cool until they let something slip and you realize they may in fact be bat-shit psycho? That's how Lou Ford, the protagonist of The Killer Inside Me is. I also suspect that Jim Thompson may have been that way as well.<br/><br/>The Killer Inside Me is the story of Lou Ford, a small town sheriff who's a little slow and a little boring. Or he would have you believe. Lou Ford spends most of his time keeping <i>the sickness</i> inside him in check. Lou's a sociopa</span>
  <span id="freeText7215691641152538913" style="display:none">Ever meet someone at a party and think they're pretty cool until they let something slip and you realize they may in fact be bat-shit psycho? That's how Lou Ford, the protagonist of The Killer Inside Me is. I also suspect that Jim Thompson may have been that way as well.<br /><br />The Killer Inside Me is the story of Lou Ford, a small town sheriff who's a little slow and a little boring. Or he would have you believe. Lou Ford spends most of his time keeping <i>the sickness</i> inside him in check. Lou's a sociopath and has killed multiple times in the past. Lou tries to get even with a man he suspects killed his brother and gets himself ensnared in a criminal investigation. Can he murder his way out of it?<br /><br />The story itself is pretty simple. Ford tries to set something up to sully the good name of the Conway family and chaos ensues. What makes it work is Jim Thompson's writing. Just like in Population 1280, Jim Thompson uses an unreliable narrator and plays it to the hilt. The writing is bleak, powerful, and unsettling. Like I said earlier, Thompson writes sociopaths a little too well for comfort. Sometimes you wish you could warn the characters that Lou Ford is a runaway train and they're standing on the track.<br /><br />From beginning to end, this was one of the more disturbing books I've ever read. If you like noir, it doesn't get much noir-er then this.<br /><br />"You've got forever; and it's a mile wide and an inch deep and full of alligators."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7215691641152538913" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_381319656" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/381319656" />
    <a title="Trudi" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi"><img alt="Trudi" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1446613110p2/1416912.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/381319656?book_show_action=true">Jul 31, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trudi" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trudi" href="/user/show/1416912-trudi">Trudi</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316404068" href="/book/show/18129494-the-killer-inside-me">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=noir-is-the-new-black">noir-is-the-new-black</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=2015">2015</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=pulpy-goodness">pulpy-goodness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=heart-of-darkness">heart-of-darkness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=stephen-king-recommends">stephen-king-recommends</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1416912-trudi?shelf=good-country-people">good-country-people</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer381319656" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer563424079654539486"><br/>First of all, a warning: if you happen to pick up the edition I did that includes an introductory essay from Stephen King, make sure you read it <i>after</i> you finish the book. Goddamn it, either the entire principal of *spoiler* completely flies over this man's head, or he just loves being a bastard about these things. After <a href='http://www.popmythology.com/stephen-king-tweets-got-spoiler-invokes-wrath-of-twitter-again/' rel='nofollow noopener'>2014's Twitter controversy</a> where he spoiled a major death for fans of HBO's <i>Game of Thrones</i> series, I'm pretty certain it's the latter. <br/><br/>It's not that he doesn't get it -- he jus</span>
  <span id="freeText563424079654539486" style="display:none"><br>First of all, a warning: if you happen to pick up the edition I did that includes an introductory essay from Stephen King, make sure you read it <i>after</i> you finish the book. Goddamn it, either the entire principal of *spoiler* completely flies over this man's head, or he just loves being a bastard about these things. After <a href="http://www.popmythology.com/stephen-king-tweets-got-spoiler-invokes-wrath-of-twitter-again/" rel="nofollow noopener">2014's Twitter controversy</a> where he spoiled a major death for fans of HBO's <i>Game of Thrones</i> series, I'm pretty certain it's the latter. <br><br>It's not that he doesn't get it -- he just doesn't care!!! <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1421803266i/13417848.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>And he does it here too, spoiling a MAJOR scene from Thompson's classic noir novel. Thanks a lot, Uncle Stevie!!! I don't care that the book was published in 1952 -- it's not the same as revealing the Titanic hits an iceberg and sinks or that Janet Leigh gets stabbed in the shower in Psycho! And it's <i>especially</i> not the same as <a href="https://twitter.com/stephenking/status/455533518685487105" rel="nofollow noopener">revealing that Romeo and Juliet die in Act 5</a>. Now you're just being an asshole, asshole! <br><br>Anyway, all wrath and chagrin aside, Uncle Stevie gives great introduction (heh) and this essay is particularly inspired dealing as it does with Jim Thompson, his mark on dark literature, and the enduring legacy of his psychopathic, unassuming small town Deputy Sheriff, Lou Ford.<br><br>Told in the first-person, <i>The Killer Inside Me</i> is as close as you're ever going to want to get to the inner thoughts and irrepressible urges of a psycho killer. The most chilling part? On the outside, Lou Ford is a regular, down home good ol' boy, with charm and even some wit. But underneath his methodically constructed facade lurks a steel-trap mind and inexplicable violent compulsions. First published in 1952, I can only imagine the impact this book would have had on its original audience. Even to this jaded 21st century reader <i>The Killer Inside Me</i> still holds within its ruthless prose the power to shock and unsettle. <br><br>And despite Ford's obvious dark passenger -- his "sickness" -- you still find yourself rooting for the guy (that is when you're not screaming at characters to run for their fucking lives far, far away from the crazy man). It made me consider who I'd take my chances with in a locked room -- Lou Ford or Annie Wilkes? ::shudder:: There's a Sophie's Choice I'm glad I never have to make. <br><br>Without Jim Thompson -- and especially without Lou Ford -- I can only believe <a href="https://bustybookbimbo.wordpress.com/2013/04/24/going-up-the-country-the-rise-of-rural-lit-and-why-you-need-to-be-reading-it/" rel="nofollow noopener">'country noir'</a> would not be what it is today. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/784866.Donald_Ray_Pollock" title="Donald Ray Pollock" rel="nofollow noopener">Donald Ray Pollock</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3983305.Frank_Bill" title="Frank Bill" rel="nofollow noopener">Frank Bill</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/65135.Daniel_Woodrell" title="Daniel Woodrell" rel="nofollow noopener">Daniel Woodrell</a>, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/24820.Ron_Rash" title="Ron Rash" rel="nofollow noopener">Ron Rash</a> all owe a debt to Thompson. And as readers, so do we. </span>
  <a data-text-id="563424079654539486" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_148838659" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/148838659" />
    <a title="Stephen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen"><img alt="Stephen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1311616811p2/1413439.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/148838659?book_show_action=true">Feb 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Stephen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Stephen" href="/user/show/1413439-stephen">Stephen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=hardboiled-and-noir">hardboiled-and-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1413439-stephen?shelf=psychos">psychos</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer148838659" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10548470863731900023"><b>HOWDY FOLKS…MY NAME IS LOU FORD AND I’M A MILD-MANNERED, DEPUTY SHERIFF IN A SMALL TEXAS TOWN...OH BTW...I’M ALSO A SADISTIC, DEEPLY DISTURBED, PSYCHOPATHIC KILLER....NICE TO MEET YOU!!</b><br/><br/><a href='http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&current=killer-inside-me-2.jpg' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380345807i/700406.jpg' alt='Photobucket' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>5.0 stars. A “one of a kind” reading experience that I can not recommend more highly for fans of noir crime fiction or psychological thrillers. Told in the first person by Lou Ford, who to all outward appearances is a thoughtful, considerate (if somewhat slow) Deputy Sheriff of Capital City, Texas, population </span>
  <span id="freeText10548470863731900023" style="display:none"><b>HOWDY FOLKS…MY NAME IS LOU FORD AND I’M A MILD-MANNERED, DEPUTY SHERIFF IN A SMALL TEXAS TOWN...OH BTW...I’M ALSO A SADISTIC, DEEPLY DISTURBED, PSYCHOPATHIC KILLER....NICE TO MEET YOU!!</b><br><br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=killer-inside-me-2.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380345807i/700406.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>5.0 stars. A “one of a kind” reading experience that I can not recommend more highly for fans of noir crime fiction or psychological thrillers. Told in the first person by Lou Ford, who to all outward appearances is a thoughtful, considerate (if somewhat slow) Deputy Sheriff of Capital City, Texas, population 50,000. Lou never gets mad, doesn’t even carry a gun and seems to be the ideal law enforcement officer given his even keel and ability to handle almost any situation. <br><br>Well don’t get too comfortable because there has never been a better example of the old adage, “looks can be deceiving.” Inside Lou is a cold, calculating killer with absolutely ZERO empathy for the people around him who he sees as simpletons and worthless. In fact, Lou’s practiced “down home” manner and slow way of talking is part of the “<b>GAME</b>” he plays with people so that they will never know who he really is. This man will make your skin crawl right off your body. <br><br>I don’t want to spoiler plot details so I will just mention a few things about Jim Thompson’s writing which I thought was PERFECT PERSONIFIED. The author, through his disturbed protagonist, takes us along on the killer’s journey, seeing everything through the lens of his warped worldview and so we are not seeing a view of his actions as wrong but as he sees them (i.e., the completely justified actions of a deranged mind). It is a unique experience to say the least. <br><br>The deftness and nuance of the writing was amazing. Throughout the story, we are given subtle clues and snippets of information that explain to the reader that Lou has had “the sickness” as he calls it since he was 15 years old, when he brutally attacked and killed a little girl. The two things I found most chilling about the story were (1) the complete lack of emotion on the part of Lou as he describes <b>truly despicable</b> acts as if they simply had to be done and (2) his outwardly pleasant demeanor and interaction with the residents in the town while we are aware of how he despises the world around him. <br><br>It reminded me a little of Michael Rooker’s excellent performance in the truly disturbing movie, <i>“Henry: Portrait of a Serial Killer”</i>, though Jim Thompson’s writing is much more subtle and nuanced. However, both that film and this book did a great job of creating a constant and ever present sense of “dread” in which the audience is aware that even though everything seems normal, it really isn’t and are left waiting for something to go horribly, horribly wrong.<br><br>This book will crawl inside you and make you feel like........<br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Confused%20and%20surprised/?action=view&amp;current=olaf-screaming-l.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380316471i/689286.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>Brilliantly written and deftly plotted, this is certainly worthy of the praise Stanley Kubrick gave it when he said, <b>“Probably the most chilling and believable first-person story of a criminally warped mind I have ever encountered.”</b><br><br><a href="http://s153.photobucket.com/albums/s207/sullypython/Goodreads/?action=view&amp;current=leonidasthinksyurcool-1.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380345807i/700407.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="10548470863731900023" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_109963251" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/109963251" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/109963251?book_show_action=true">Jul 04, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=serial-killers">serial-killers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=crime-mystery">crime-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=famous-books">famous-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=noir">noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer109963251" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7752516715441866092">Jim Thompson must have had noir in his veins instead of red blood cells. This dark first-person story has the reader inhabiting the mind of a killer in way that most authors can't even come close to matching. It's disturbing, chilling and one of the best pieces of crime fiction I’ve ever read.<br/><br/>Lou Ford is a small-town sheriff’s deputy in West Texas. He appears to be just a good natured, not-to-bright, good-ole-boy who usually speaks in a series of clichés to the point of annoying or boring whoeve</span>
  <span id="freeText7752516715441866092" style="display:none">Jim Thompson must have had noir in his veins instead of red blood cells. This dark first-person story has the reader inhabiting the mind of a killer in way that most authors can't even come close to matching. It's disturbing, chilling and one of the best pieces of crime fiction I’ve ever read.<br /><br />Lou Ford is a small-town sheriff’s deputy in West Texas. He appears to be just a good natured, not-to-bright, good-ole-boy who usually speaks in a series of clichés to the point of annoying or boring whoever he’s talking to. But Lou’s persona is all a mask to hide his true self and to keep what he thinks of as ‘his sickness’ in check.<br /><br />When Lou is dispatched to give a warning to a call-girl named Joyce, it escalates into a confrontation that unleashes Lou’s sadistic side, and he’s shocked to discover that Joyce is a willing partner. Letting his darker impulses out of the box soon leads Lou to more violence, and then a lengthy cat-and-mouse game with the local power structure as he covers up his crimes with a mixture of his dimwitted persona and even more bloodshed.<br /><br />Reading this is a really odd experience. At times, you find yourself rooting for Lou to get away with everything he’s done, but at other times you want to scream at the other characters, “Run! He’s freaking crazier than a shithouse rat! Get out of there before he murders you all!” <br /><br />And I was both horrified and amused at the malicious joy that Lou takes in ‘needling’ people under the guise of playing the fool that can’t stop running his mouth. He’s got a knack for annoying and insulting people while he pretends he doesn’t realize what he’s doing. That’s just one of the many ways that evil Lou has of getting under your skin. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7752516715441866092" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/397514713?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer397514713" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3419112204749454293">I went into this with high expectations. I mean, who doesn't love a good psychopath? Especially one with a boat-load of issues who is in a position of authority and trust. Enter Lou Ford, small town sheriff and all-round good guy... or so his sweet and slightly slow disposition would have you think. But Lou has <i>the sickness</i>. Most of the time he manages to keep it hidden beneath a cheery and easy-going attitude, most of the time you would assume he is just your average Joe. Until every once in a </span>
  <span id="freeText3419112204749454293" style="display:none">I went into this with high expectations. I mean, who doesn't love a good psychopath? Especially one with a boat-load of issues who is in a position of authority and trust. Enter Lou Ford, small town sheriff and all-round good guy... or so his sweet and slightly slow disposition would have you think. But Lou has <i>the sickness</i>. Most of the time he manages to keep it hidden beneath a cheery and easy-going attitude, most of the time you would assume he is just your average Joe. Until every once in a while his temper rises and he becomes possessed with an uncontrollable rage... and the killer inside him comes out.<br /><br />Fabulous. Except for the part where I was rather disappointed. <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/298663.The_Killer_Inside_Me" title="The Killer Inside Me by Jim Thompson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Killer Inside Me</a> isn't a bad book, it was just nowhere near as good as I thought it was going to be. Lou Ford did not creep me out in the way he was supposed to, he didn't creep me out in the way <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/3389.Stephen_King" title="Stephen King" rel="nofollow noopener">Stephen King</a> and Stanley Kubrick claim to have been creeped out by him. Dolores Umbridge is creepier than Lou.</span>

<div id="review_300426808" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/300426808" />
    <a title="Lawyer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer"><img alt="Lawyer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603672493p2/3025547.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/300426808?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lawyer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lawyer" href="/user/show/3025547-lawyer">Lawyer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anyone</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lawyer by: </span>
      Members of goodreads group Pulp Fiction
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=20th-century">20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=crime">crime</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=fathers-and-sons">fathers-and-sons</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=anti-social-personality">anti-social-personality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=good-and-evil">good-and-evil</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=human-behavior">human-behavior</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=sex">sex</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=killers">killers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3025547-lawyer?shelf=noir">noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer300426808" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5615981225484147212"><b>The Killer Inside Me: <i>Jim Thompson's classic Roman Noir</i></b><br/><br/><blockquote>“Just as there are physical monsters, can there not be mental or psychic monsters born? The face and body may be perfect, but if a twisted gene or malformed egg can produce physical monsters, may not the same process produce a malformed soul? <br/><br/>Monsters are variations from the accepted normal to a greater or a less degree. As a child may be born without an arm, so one may be born without kindness or the potential of conscience. A man who loses</blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText5615981225484147212" style="display:none"><b>The Killer Inside Me: <i>Jim Thompson's classic Roman Noir</i></b><br><br><blockquote>“Just as there are physical monsters, can there not be mental or psychic monsters born? The face and body may be perfect, but if a twisted gene or malformed egg can produce physical monsters, may not the same process produce a malformed soul? <br><br>Monsters are variations from the accepted normal to a greater or a less degree. As a child may be born without an arm, so one may be born without kindness or the potential of conscience. A man who loses his arms in an accident has a great struggle to adjust himself to the lack, but one born without arms suffers only from people who find him strange. Having never had arms, he cannot miss them. To a monster the norm must seem monstrous, since everyone is normal to himself. To the inner monster it must be even more obscure, since he has no visible thing to compare with others. To a criminal, honesty is foolish. You must not forget that a monster is only a variation, and that to a monster the norm is monstrous.”― <b>John Steinbeck, <i>East of Eden</i>, 1952</b>
</blockquote><br><br><a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=KillerInsideMe.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380373258i/726817.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>Faucett Crest, Paperback edition, 1952</b> <br><br><i>"A novel about murder unlike anything you've ever read."</i> There it is right on the cover of the Faucett-Crest Original. And the people at that publishing company got it right. <br><br>My grandmother had a saying it was always easy to know someone who wasn't right. "That boy just don't look right out of his eyes." And I've known the times that she was right--absolutely right. But I think Steinbeck nails it. Because you can't always tell you're dealing with a monster. Because the face and body may be perfect. They are not physical monsters, no freak at the side show at the carnival. These people walk among us, looking and acting just the way we do, day to day. But they lack something we don<a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/list/3025547?shelf=psychology't">http://www.goodreads.com/review/list/...</a>. That is conscience. That is a value for the difference between right and wrong.<br><br>Take Jim Thompson's protagonist, Lou Ford. He's a deputy sheriff. He's the go to guy when it gets down to getting someone to talk. He's a natural at it, mouthing platitudes, assuring his suspects that he's their friend. He's respected by his sheriff.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.beta.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/the-killer-inside-me-poster-old_zps678bcb15.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380373258i/726818.jpg" alt=" photo the-killer-inside-me-poster-old_zps678bcb15.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>Casey Affleck as Lou Ford in the 2010 film directed by Michael Winterbottom</i><br><br>However, when Lou feels the sickness, as he calls it coming on, he says he can't control himself. Perhaps you say Lou Ford was criminally insane. Not so. Lou knew the difference between right and wrong. He didn't give a damn. And when he determined it in his best interest, if people had to die, well, they were already dead in his book. Lou is a careful planner. He is a craftsman at construction of alibis. Adept at creating evidence pointing in anyone's direction but him, he's capable of covering his tracks well. Murder is not something that gnaws at his conscience, because he lacks one. Killing two people and covering his own skin, Lou returns home to his father's house where he prepares and wolfs down a large breakfast of ham and eggs. He's not squeamish.<br><br>I can't fault <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7621.Jim_Thompson" title="Jim Thompson" rel="nofollow noopener">Jim Thompson</a> for the psychology he cites accurately, the material that was commonly referred to at the time of his writing <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/298663.The_Killer_Inside_Me" title="The Killer Inside Me by Jim Thompson" rel="nofollow noopener">The Killer Inside Me</a> Emil Kraepelin, whose works Lou Ford studies in his father's medical library is credited with the birth of modern psychiatric diagnoses. However Ford singles out Kraepelin's work on <i>dementia praecox</i> the precursor for what we now know as Schizophrenia. That diagnosis is a psychosis, amounting to a break with reality and a failure to recognize reality. A common description of defining a person's mental status is whether he is oriented x 3, that is, to person, place and time. That does not ever fit Lou Ford. He's conscious of person place and time at all times. It's his "moral" compass that's broken.<br><br><a href="http://s1151.beta.photobucket.com/user/mmcsullivan/media/Emil_Kraepelin_1926_zps53931a3d.jpg.html" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380373258i/726819.jpg" alt=" photo Emil_Kraepelin_1926_zps53931a3d.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><i>Emil Kraepelin, Lou Ford's favorite author</i><br><br>Lou Ford's personality is described with unerring accuracy in Kraepelin's later work, which would have been available to Jim Thompson, under sections dealing with <i>moral insanity</i>. From wikipedia: <br><br><blockquote>In fact from 1904 Kraepelin changed the section heading to 'The born criminal', moving it from under 'Congenital feeblemindedness' to a new chapter on 'Psychopathic personalities'. They were treated under a theory of degeneration. Four types were distinguished: born criminals (inborn delinquents), pathological liars, querulous persons, and Triebmenschen (persons driven by a basic compulsion, including vagabonds, spendthrifts, and dipsomaniacs). The concept of 'psychopathic inferiorities' had been recently popularised in Germany by Julius Ludwig August Koch, who proposed congenital and acquired types. Kraepelin had no evidence or explanation suggesting a congenital cause, and his assumption therefore appears to have been simple 'biologism'. Others, such as Gustav Aschaffenburg, argued for a varying combination of causes. Kraepelin's assumption of a moral defect rather than a positive drive towards crime has also been questioned, as it implies that the moral sense is somehow inborn and unvarying, yet it was known to vary by time and place, and Kraepelin never considered that the moral sense might just be different. Kurt Schneider criticized Kraepelin's nosology for appearing to be a list of behaviors that he considered undesirable, rather than medical conditions, though Schneider's alternative version has also been criticised on the same basis. Nevertheless, many essentials of these diagnostic systems were introduced into the diagnostic systems, and remarkable similarities remain in the DSM-IV and ICD-10.[4] The issues would today mainly be considered under the category of personality disorders, or in terms of Kraepelin's focus <i>Antisocial/Dissocial personality disorder or psychopathy.</i> (Emphasis added)</blockquote><br><br>If there is anything in modern psychology that rings true, it deals with the development of sexuality. It is borne out be current research in the field that an adult's aberrant sexual behavior is often set during adolescence by the occurrence of a sexual event which leads the target of that event to recreate situations similar to those experienced in adolescence. So, perhaps whatever happened between Lou and his father's housekeeper, bent Lou a little crooked in his interactions with women in his adult years. And, of course, we know of his experience with a three year old girl up in the barn loft for which his foster brother took the blame. We also know that Dr. Foster knew of his son's aberrations, keeping him close under wraps, at home in Central City, Texas.<br><br>So, if you want to know what runs through the mind of a killer, Jim Thompson's novel is the one for you. Don't blame me if it sends a chill up your spine every few chapters are so. Listening to Lou Ford's story puts you across the table from Ted Bundy and John Wayne Gacy, just to name a few. And, when you're finished with this book, don't take too much comfort that it's only a story. For there are monsters that walk among us and sometimes they look just perfect out of their eyes.<br><br> <a href="http://s1151.photobucket.com/albums/o631/mmcsullivan/?action=view&amp;current=thompson.jpg" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380373258i/726820.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br><b>Jim Thompson, (September 27, 1906, Anadarko, Oklahoma Territory – April 7, 1977, Los Angeles, California)</b> </span>
  <a data-text-id="5615981225484147212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_101822668" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101822668" />
    <a title="Lou" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3699303-lou"><img alt="Lou" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1312212794p2/3699303.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101822668?book_show_action=true">May 08, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lou" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lou" href="/user/show/3699303-lou">Lou</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3699303-lou?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3699303-lou?shelf=noir">noir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101822668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9250148971805960018">This was a humdinger of a story written through the eyes of a sheriff Lou Ford of a small, middle-of-nowhere west Texas town of Central City. Is he an easy-going, well-liked man and a respected citizen of the town, well known for his quiet, gentle nature? On the inside he has a dark-side he is a sociopathic killer who seems to think that life is ruled by any means necessary, full of both corny, small-town bonhomie and murderous psychosexual rage. He will not hesitate to eliminate his loved ones </span>
  <span id="freeText9250148971805960018" style="display:none">This was a humdinger of a story written through the eyes of a sheriff Lou Ford of a small, middle-of-nowhere west Texas town of Central City. Is he an easy-going, well-liked man and a respected citizen of the town, well known for his quiet, gentle nature? On the inside he has a dark-side he is a sociopathic killer who seems to think that life is ruled by any means necessary, full of both corny, small-town bonhomie and murderous psychosexual rage. He will not hesitate to eliminate his loved ones with brutal emotional dis-attachment. In the following discussion in the novel he is describing his perspective on life.<br><b>
  <blockquote>"You're a square Joe." "am I ?" i said."How do you know i am, Johnny? How can a man ever really know anything? Were living in a funny world, kid, a peculiar civilisation. The police are playing crooks in it, and the crooks are doing police duty. The politicians are preachers, and the preachers are politicians. The tax collectors collect for themselves. The bad people want us to have more dough, and the good people are fighting to keep it from us. It's not good for us, know what i mean? If we all had all we wanted to eat, we'd crap too much. We'd have inflation in toilet paper industry. That's the way i understand it. That's about the size of some of the arguments i've heard."<br>"Theres a time of peace I said and a time of war. A time to sow and a time to reap. A time to live and a time to die"</blockquote>
</b><br>Jim Thompson creates a main protagonist that you get to like, but at the same time he has a side to him we would all hate. Thompson tries to show us that the cliched perspective of 1950s America as a land of communal benevolence and white picket fences requires attention. Paper thin, with a cancerous presence under the skin from the actions of 'evil that men do.' It is a mark of Thompson's skill that our identification with Lou, encouraged by his first-person narration, is never quite frayed. While he seems at the same time to explain his reasons for his actions he does not quite understanding why he treads that path, he tries to understand himself in his story by digging out a quote out of a psychology textbook.<br><br><b>
  <blockquote>"The subject suffers from strong feelings of guilt... combined with a sense of frustration and persecution... which increase as he grows older; yet there are rarely if ever any surface signs of... disturbance. On the contrary, his behaviour appears to be entirely logical. He reasons soundly, even shrewdly. He is completely aware of what he does and why he does it..."</blockquote>
</b><br><br>This was my first Thompson novel and made me dig out his other works I found them all to just as hard boiled and compelling. The movie adaptation recently was good but had some brutal images.<br><a target="_blank" href="http://s1184.photobucket.com/albums/z322/LouPendergrast/Killer%20Inside%20Me/?action=view&amp;current=TheKillerinsidemefront.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380334712i/693973._SY540_.jpg" alt="Photobucket" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br>Also on my webpage @<a href="http://more2read.com/review/the-killer-inside-me-by-jim-thompson/" rel="nofollow noopener">http://more2read.com/review/the-killer-inside-me-by-jim-thompson/</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="9250148971805960018" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_2004522475" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2004522475" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2004522475?book_show_action=true">May 21, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780316404068" href="/book/show/18129494-the-killer-inside-me">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=books-loved-2017">books-loved-2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=mystery-detective-thriller">mystery-detective-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=fiction-20th-century">fiction-20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2004522475" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12338755599071172534">I had not before this read any work by Jim Thompson, though I knew his reputation as a dark and grisly noir novelist from the mid- twentieth century. I had seen The Grifters (which I loved), but never read the book on which it was based (but now will!). I had recently read a comics adaptation of The Killer Inside, and liked it, so committed to reading the original, which I really did basically love. <br/><br/>Well, “love” is perhaps a simplistic stretch of an assessment for my experience of a serial kille</span>
  <span id="freeText12338755599071172534" style="display:none">I had not before this read any work by Jim Thompson, though I knew his reputation as a dark and grisly noir novelist from the mid- twentieth century. I had seen The Grifters (which I loved), but never read the book on which it was based (but now will!). I had recently read a comics adaptation of The Killer Inside, and liked it, so committed to reading the original, which I really did basically love. <br /><br />Well, “love” is perhaps a simplistic stretch of an assessment for my experience of a serial killer’s first person account. It reminds me a little bit of Lolita, or Satan, in Milton’s Paradise Lost; in all three of these works you have elegantly written depictions of articulate monsters who convince many others (including many readers!) they are charming and admirable. <br /><br />The Killer Inside Me is the story of Lou Ford, a small town (Central City) deputy sheriff who is straight-laced and on the surface unremarkable. He has girlfriends, he has other friends, he does good and responsible work, but he allows no one to get close. Lou grew up with a sickness that he wants to control. He’s articulate, he’s well read, but he’s also a sociopath who has killed many people, and no one suspects anything about him. So can he cure his own sickness? The route he takes to mental health, well, let’s just say he may have benefitted just a bit from some professional help instead of just going that self-help route. Mistakes were definitely made going down that road.<br /><br />The book has some terrific writing, it is deservedly a classic of the genre, but there are some (I warn you) disturbing revelations in this book, which he details even as he talks about the weather and so on in a very calm fashion. He’s seemingly unaffected by what he does. For instance, he seems to care very much for a young man, Johnnie, Lou listens to him, he counsels him, but is finally willing to throw him under the bus (Okay, not literally).<br /><br />There’s some speculation by the narrator himself about what makes him tick, and we are naturally curious about all that. Why does he do it? Is it the result of some early shock? Is it the fact that his mother died when he was young? That he got a step-brother early on, or is it the loss of his step-brother from murder? Lou’s Dad had scores of books on religion and psychology around the house, and Lou read many of them; the presumption is that moral and psychological explanations were sought, once Dad decided Lou had a “problem,” but nowhere in this book is a satisfactory explanation given for Lou’s horrific behavior. He has almost no real insight into himself, which is one really chilling thing about him, of course. At one point Lou says, “There are things that have to be forgotten if you want to go on living,” but he only hints just what it is that needs to be forgotten. He does terrible things and seems to forget them right away!<br /><br />There's a fascinating exchange between Lou and his pragmatic lawyer about whether anyone can accurately be identified as evil. The lawyer says, "The name you give a thing depends on where you are standing. A weed is just a plant out of place." This kind of relativistic thinking is twisted by both of these guys to their own advantage. <br /><br />At one point the oblivious monster Lou philosophizes to the young Johnnie: “’Yeah, Johnnie,’ I said, ‘it’s a screwed up, bitched up world, and I’m afraid it’s going to stay that way. And I’ll tell you why. Because no one, almost no one, sees anything wrong with it. They can’t see that things are screwed up, so they’re not worried about it. <br /> <br />We're living in a funny world kid, a peculiar civilization. The police are playing crooks in it, and the crooks are doing police duty. The politicians are preachers, and the preachers are politicians. The tax collectors collect for themselves. The Bad People want us to have more dough, and the good people are fighting to keep it from us. It's not good for us, know what I mean?’”<br /><br />You might wonder why a killer would write his own morbid story. So periodically he talks about the process of writing his (fictional) “memoir.” For instance:<br /><br />“In lots of books I read, the writer seems to go haywire every time he reaches a high point. He’ll start leaving out punctuation and running his words together and babble about stars flashing and sinking into a deep dreamless sea. And you can’t figure out whether the hero’s laying his girl or a cornerstone. I guess that kind of crap is supposed to be pretty deep stuff—a lot of the book reviewers eat it up, I notice. But the way I see it is, the writer is just too goddam lazy to do his job. And I’m not lazy, whatever else I am. I’ll tell you everything.” <br /><br />Oh, and he does, I warn you. I do recommend it, but it's disturbing in places though at the same time very well-written. But I have to go back to reading Agatha Christie for awhile. This was a pretty intense! </span>
  <a data-text-id="12338755599071172534" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_272092642" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/272092642" />
    <a title="knig" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6163905-knig"><img alt="knig" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_50x66-6a03a5c12233c941481992b82eea8d23.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/272092642?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="knig" class="user" itemprop="url" name="knig" href="/user/show/6163905-knig">knig</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to knig by: </span>
      Steve Kendall
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163905-knig?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6163905-knig?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer272092642" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7519315416788073447">When Boris Vian hoaxed his way into the roman noir scene in 1958 with ‘I spit on your graves’, he was giving Jim Thompson a nod.<br/><br/>This book is riveting. It springs on the back of Chandler and Hammet who were by then moulding the no-nonsense, cynical, take no prisoner ‘Has- Been’ into limelight situations, but whereas these pioneer anti-heroes seem to preserve a modicum of decency, their successors, guided by the likes of Patricia Highsmith, Vian and Thompson seem to surgically remove that modicum,</span>
  <span id="freeText7519315416788073447" style="display:none">When Boris Vian hoaxed his way into the roman noir scene in 1958 with ‘I spit on your graves’, he was giving Jim Thompson a nod.<br /><br />This book is riveting. It springs on the back of Chandler and Hammet who were by then moulding the no-nonsense, cynical, take no prisoner ‘Has- Been’ into limelight situations, but whereas these pioneer anti-heroes seem to preserve a modicum of decency, their successors, guided by the likes of Patricia Highsmith, Vian and Thompson seem to surgically remove that modicum, leaving in its wake a macabre portrait of pathology. <br /><br />General consensus ties our current era of rudderless moral drift into incoherent and incongruous direction to the sounding knell of overt rebelliousness in the 1960s, but it seems, in fact, a lot of groundwork was being laid quietly but decisively in the 50s. Its writers like Thompson who broke with established zeitgeist in the 50s and kickstarted the momentum culminating in the total anti-thesis of the status quo in the 60s.<br /><br /> Lou Ford is the quintessential psychopath, a bogeyman: he gives me the shivers across a fifty year divide, he is that fresh. And relevant. The full horror of this man is the utter unpredictability of a pillar in society jeckyll-n’hyding into a psychotic killer, with no warning signs to give the community at large an inkling as to the monster lurking inside.<br /><br />Lou is a damaged, sad human being who epitomises, on some level, the universal sense of guilt imbued in all of us who are products of the 2000 year old judeo-christian legacy, and particularly in relation to sex (bearing in mind this novel came out in the 1950s). Psychologically gripping and disturbing, this book is unputdownable.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7519315416788073447" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_72631533" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72631533" />
    <a title="notgettingenough " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough"><img alt="notgettingenough " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1258115161p2/2658311.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/72631533?book_show_action=true">Sep 27, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="notgettingenough " class="user" itemprop="url" name="notgettingenough " href="/user/show/2658311-notgettingenough">notgettingenough </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2658311-notgettingenough?shelf=modern-lit">modern-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer72631533" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5946491985846033736">This was my orginal thoughts with which I was never satisfied:<br/><br/><br/>Until I saw this my gut feeling was that it would be impossible to take Jim Thompson to the screen, but I stand corrected. Fabulous movie which precisely captures the spirit of Thompson’s writing. I first suggested seeing this to a male who refused on the grounds that ‘horrible things happened to women’ and they do, but I have no idea why this would be interpreted as being about ‘male hate’ ‘misogeny’. Like most people, I guess, my re</span>
  <span id="freeText5946491985846033736" style="display:none">This was my orginal thoughts with which I was never satisfied:<br /><br /><br />Until I saw this my gut feeling was that it would be impossible to take Jim Thompson to the screen, but I stand corrected. Fabulous movie which precisely captures the spirit of Thompson’s writing. I first suggested seeing this to a male who refused on the grounds that ‘horrible things happened to women’ and they do, but I have no idea why this would be interpreted as being about ‘male hate’ ‘misogeny’. Like most people, I guess, my reactions are that although at an intellectual level extreme violence against men is as dreadful as against women, at an emotional level that simply isn’t so. However, I can’t see that this movie is any more visually violent than, say, Pan’s Labyrinth and Red Riding Trilogy, the violence being sickening in both. In both of these I recall violence against men. I don’t think violence like this should ever be shown as ‘entertainment’. It diminishes the nature of violence, it does desensitise, it does make it normal, even as we complain about it.<br /><br />And this to me brings to mind a discussion I started in my review of Stendhal’s <i>Memoirs of an Egotist</i>. <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/116549813">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a><br /><br />There we were talking about the fact that a picture can give an impression which if read instead would be found cheap and coarse. At the time I suggested that the reverse would surely sometimes be true, that a nasty picture could be ennnobled by a description in words and this kept coming back to me in the movie. One of the things Thompson does is describe violence in the most gripping, gut-wrenching way which makes one feel there and part of it. I say that as one who finds descriptions of violence generally tedious, both visual and by word. His writing of this kind of thing is staggeringly good. And although I haven’t read this book yet, I’ve read enough Jim Thompson to be sure that the scenes where Winterbottom attempts to force us to watch women (as it happens) being punched and kicked to death, would have been utterly readable in a way they were not - and indeed should not have been - watchable. However real Thompson’s descriptions are, they still have not been robbed of the reader’s imagination in the way film steals. I wish more film directors understood that suggestion is so much more powerful than blatancy. Strangely, I think the way to transfer to the screen what I expect to have been the explicit nature of Thompson’s description of these scenes would have been to draw back from the explicit. Maybe this is because in the end, in a movie, you are watching rather than taking part in the way you are when reading.<br /><br />Jim Thompson, out of favour for decades, has suddenly become flavour of the month, his books are back in mainstream print and now this movie. All I can say is that he should never have been out of fashion, he is a splendid writer and I don’t want to put a genre on that any more than I would on Simenon’s non-Maigret books. They are part of a movement of mid-to-late-twentieth century studies of sociopaths which are, in my opinion, a very important part of the literature of that period. So get trendy and read him…and yes, by all means see the movie too.<br /><br />End of initial thoughts. Having taken these off this review, the discussion <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72519807">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a> with Paul prompts me to repost.<br /><br />And express a reconsideration. In retrospect, I consider the way in which the violence was portrayed here to be absolutely legitimate. Maybe there are other ways of doing it that would have worked. I think of the film The Boys in which there is almost no explicit violence and yet the threat looms far larger than the execution. But still, in order to get inside the head of the killer I can see that the approach taken by the director maybe worked in a way that was utterly horrific but still meaningful. I do not think that of either Pan's Labyrinth or Red Riding Trilogy where the violence served no purpose whatsoever.<br /><br />-------------------<br /><br />I feel like I've failed this book, so I'm starting again...<br /><br />It was watching the movie of this book that gave me one of those moments of understanding.<br /><br />There are the ones who say what they believe, who say what they mean. Then there are the ones who believe what they say, who mean what they say. This second group is convinced that their very act of saying something makes it true. ‘I’ve said it, therefore I mean it, therefore it is true.’<br /><br />The – I really don’t know what to call him, villain??? – kicks and punches to death a woman. He explains as he is doing it that he has to do it, it cannot be helped and, of course, he has said it, therefore in his view of the world, it is true. In a deeply moving moment as the woman is lying on the floor, dying, a gentle pool of her urine growing on the floor, she reaches for her handbag. Why? Is there something with which to belatedly defend herself in there? Her hand doesn’t make it. She dies first. Later we find that she was reaching to find a letter she had for this man, her love. I suspect some critics thought we were supposed to see this woman as weak, not putting up any resistance as she was so brutally assaulted, but they don’t get it. She loved the man who was kicking her to death. Not at any point did that love waver. It was strength, not weakness that we witnessed in this scene. She loved this man. She wanted to deliver her letter.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5946491985846033736" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Carol" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7668522-carol"><img alt="Carol" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1546364878p2/7668522.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/678196559?book_show_action=true">Jul 29, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carol" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carol" href="/user/show/7668522-carol">Carol</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=classics">classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=kindle">kindle</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=dark">dark</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=ebook">ebook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=horror">horror</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=library-overdrive">library-overdrive</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=crime-noir">crime-noir</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7668522-carol?shelf=stephen-king-recommends">stephen-king-recommends</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer678196559" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8567912780401932251"><b>WARNING #1 -</b> The Stephen King foreward has <b>major</b> spoilers. <b>(read after)</b><p><b>WARNING #2 -</b> "Hitchhikers<b>*</b> may be escaped lunatics."</p><p>Let's begin with a Stephen King quote: <b>"THE KILLER INSIDE ME is an American classic, no less, a novel that deserves space on the same shelf as Moby-Dick, Huckleberry Finn, The Sun Also Rises, and As I Lay Dying. Thompson's other books are either good or almost great, but all of them pale before the horrifying, mesmerizing story of Lou Ford, that smiling good ol' Texas boy wh</b></p></span>
  <span id="freeText8567912780401932251" style="display:none"><b>WARNING #1 -</b> The Stephen King foreward has <b>major</b> spoilers. <b>(read after)</b><p><b>WARNING #2 -</b> "Hitchhikers<b>*</b> may be escaped lunatics."</p><p>Let's begin with a Stephen King quote: <b>"THE KILLER INSIDE ME is an American classic, no less, a novel that deserves space on the same shelf as Moby-Dick, Huckleberry Finn, The Sun Also Rises, and As I Lay Dying. Thompson's other books are either good or almost great, but all of them pale before the horrifying, mesmerizing story of Lou Ford, that smiling good ol' Texas boy who would rather beat you to death with cliches than shoot you with a .44 ...but if the cliches don't do the job, he is not afraid to pick up the gun. And use it."</b></p><p>Lou Ford is a deputy sheriff in the offbeat town of Central City, Texas. He is easy-going, soft hearted and genuinely (?) likes people. He has a pretty chatter-mouth girlfriend who he sort of loves, but will never marry, and an even prettier strumpet on the side that he claims to truly love. He likes bantering with people <i>(to drive them crazy)</i> and does indeed know how to handle troublemakers. He even likes good ol' sheriff Bob.</p><p><b>BUT....</b>Lou is also a deeply disturbed, sneaky, conniving serial killer....a truly scary type of serial killer who struts down the street in his Stetson with a satisfying smile on his face after committing an atrocious act, <b>AND,</b> <i>(as you will see)</i> his shocking comments and thoroughly disgusting laugh-out-loud reaction afterward just takes the mind-boggling cake!</p><p>The first person narration works so well here giving the reader a glimpse into the mind of a psycho killer as he plans every move, and make no doubt about it, this dude eliminates anyone who gets in his way.</p><p>First published March 13, 1952 <b>THE KILLER INSIDE ME</b> classic is an unsettling, but notable work of crime-noir-horror that I must rate 5 Stars. It's just so....what?....<b>indescribably dark and nasty?....or crazy good?</b></p><p>
  <b>*</b>
  <i>(I can't believe how many times we picked up hitchhikers in the 60's!)</i>
</p></span>
